Using Xpert Search

Xpert Search allows you to use a powerful blend of free-text and structured queries to find logs or event data from any server or client Xpert is monitoring.

Scoping

Xpert Search can scope your search down to only results for a specific node in the Xpert model. This makes it easier to find exactly what you're looking for, by selecting your service or client event in the tree at the left. However, if you're not sure what services or events were involved in what you're looking for, you're always free to search at the environment level (Prod, NonProd, etc).

Free-Text Queries

The simplest way to use Xpert Search is with free-text queries. These queries are ones that look like something you'd use with bing or the "Find" tool in notepad. For instance, if you were looking for logs containing the text "batman", you could use the query: 
Batman
Or if you're hoping to find two words in a row, something like this query with quotes would do: 
"Xbox One"
You can also use simple boolean operations with free-text search like so: 
xpert AND (fantastic OR awesome)
And you can even search for results with one word, but not another. For example, exceptions that aren't timeouts: 
exception -timeout
Wildcards are also supported, but only if the entire query is a wildcard: 
*
Or if the wildcard is at the end of a word: 
API*

Structured Queries

Xpert Search also supports structured queries that allow you to filter your search results with much more complex expressions and transforms.

Variables

To perform a structured query search, you use the "@" symbol to reference "variables" in logs or client data you're searching. Xpert will then use some magic to parse and extract the value for your variable, regardless of the structure of your data.

The simplest and most useful example of this is client events that use the Common Schema structure. Say we have client events like the below "APIF" event: 

{
    "ms": { ... }
    "APIF": {
        ...
            "LatencyMs": 3250,
            "Method": "GET",
            "Retries": 3,
            "ResponseContentLength": 1050,
        ...
    }
}
With an event like this, we can reference variables like @LatencyMs, @Method, @Retries or @ResponseContentLength.

Constraints

Now that we can reference specific values in search results by name, we're free to add more complex expressions to constrain which results we want returned. These constraint expressions always come after a free-text query portion that filters your search results first.

For instance, if we were looking only for API failed events for GET apis, that had been retried at least 3 times, we could do: 

APIF and @Method == "GET" and @Retries >= 3

You can even use variables on both sides of an expression. What if I was looking for successful API events where the latency was higher than 1 second per kilobyte? 

APIS and (@LatencyMs / 1000) > (@ResponseContentLength / 1024)

Selects

You can also use variables to perform select statements on your search results. This allows you to very quickly find just the information you're looking for, without having to visually parse tons of text. Only the variables you select will be pulled out and visualized as columns in a grid of data.

For instance, we can look for failed GET apis, and show only a few pieces of data we're curious about: 

APIF and @Method == "GET"
select @LatencyMs, @Retries, @ResponseContentLength
Which will return something like:

Of course, you can also select complex expressions instead of variables directly: 

APIF and @Method == "GET"
select @LatencyMs, @Retries, @ResponseContentLength, ((@LatencyMs / 1000) > (@ResponseContentLength / 1024)) as TooSlow
Which will return another column with the result of your expression:

Intellisense

Xpert Search uses a rich query input system that allows for awesome things like autocompletion and intellisense.

For instance, every time you do a search, we parse any Common Schema events returned and extract all possible variable names. These are then added to intellisense and will show up as soon as you type "@" so that you won't have to guess them:

We also extract any previously seen values you might want to compare your variables to. For instance, adding an equality constraint to the @Method variable in APIF suggests the following values:
